## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: classical mechanics (nonlinear oscillations / dynamical systems)
**Analysis**: The target dv_dt is acceleration, with state variables x (position) and v (velocity), which matches second-order ODE oscillator models rewritten as a first-order system. Typical structures are restoring forces as functions of x, damping as functions of v (often nonlinear), and optional time-dependent forcing/parametric excitation via t.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Linear undamped oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x (simple Hooke-type restoring acceleration, sign absorbed in c0)
- Linear viscous damping: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v (mass-spring-damper in first-order form)
- Time-harmonic forcing: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * cos(c3 * t) (externally driven oscillator)
- General conservative oscillator (potential expansion): dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 (nonlinear stiffness via cubic term)
- Duffing oscillator (damped, driven): dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * v + c3 * cos(c4 * t) (hardening/softening spring with forcing)
- Quadratic/cubic drag damping: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * v * Abs(v) (aerodynamic-like damping)
- Nonlinear (amplitude-dependent) damping: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * x**2 * v (damping grows with displacement amplitude)
- Van der Pol oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * (c2 - x**2) * v (self-excited oscillation with nonlinear damping)
- Rayleigh oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * v**3 (velocity-based nonlinear damping leading to limit cycles)
- Parametric excitation (Mathieu-type): dv_dt = (c0 + c1 * cos(c2 * t)) * x + c3 * v (time-varying stiffness)
- Pendulum small-to-moderate angle model: dv_dt = c0 * sin(c1 * x) + c2 * v (nonlinear restoring via sine)
- Piecewise/impact-type stiffness: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * Max(x - c3, 0) (contact/stop effects in oscillators)
- Polynomial-in-state normal form: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * x**3 + c3 * x**2 * v + c4 * x * v**2 + c5 * v**3 (generic low-order nonlinear oscillator expansion)
- Energy-preserving restoring from potential derivative: dv_dt = -(c0 + c1 * x**2 + c2 * x**4) * x (odd restoring force from even potential terms)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Odd restoring symmetry: if the system is symmetric under x -> -x, expect dv_dt to contain odd functions of x (x, x**3, sin(c*x)) and avoid even-only x terms.
- Conservative vs dissipative signature: conservative models often look like dv_dt = f(x) with no explicit v term; presence of v (especially with negative coefficient) indicates damping, while (c - x**2)*v indicates self-excitation (limit cycle).
- Hardening/softening stiffness: an x**3 term in dv_dt shifts frequency with amplitude; its sign distinguishes hardening vs softening behavior.
- Velocity sign-dependent dissipation: terms like v*Abs(v) (or higher odd powers v**3) indicate drag where dissipation increases strongly with speed.
- Parametric pumping: multiplicative time dependence with x, e.g., (c0 + c1*cos(c2*t))*x, suggests a time-varying stiffness rather than an external force term added independently of x.
- External forcing vs state feedback: additive cos(c*t) in dv_dt implies external drive; coupling like x*cos(c*t) implies parametric excitation; coupling like v*cos(c*t) implies modulated damping.
- Limit-cycle generators: nonlinear damping forms (c0 - x**2)*v (Van der Pol) or v - v**3 (Rayleigh-type) are canonical patterns producing stable oscillation amplitudes.
- Non-smooth dynamics indicators: Max(x-c,0) or Abs(x) terms suggest impacts, mechanical stops, or Coulomb-like effects and often create waveform distortion/harmonics.

- `-0.433424893195866*v + 0.433424893195866*v*x**2 - 3.0564119369598*x*0.0825581177584772*Abs(v)**0.333333333333333 - 3.0564119369598*x + 0.266068919820644*sin(t)`

So GT = `c_a*x + c_b*v + c_c*v*x² + c_d*sin(t) + c_e*x*|v|^(1/3)`

Where:
- `c_a = -3.0564119369598` (coefficient of x)
- `c_b = -0.433424893195866` (coefficient of v)
- `c_c = 0.433424893195866` (coefficient of v*x²)
- `c_d = 0.266068919820644` (coefficient of sin(t))
- `c_e = -0.252378...` (coefficient of x*|v|^(1/3))

The GT has these basis functions: **x, v, v·x², sin(t), x·|v|^(1/3)**

Note: sin(t) = cos(t + c) with appropriate phase shift c.

Now I'll check each candidate for whether it can express exactly these basis functions.

The key unique term is **x·|v|^(1/3)**. This is a very specific nonlinear function. Let me check which candidates could potentially represent this.

**Candidates with `x*log(c*Abs(v) + 1)`:** Candidates 1-7, 5. `log(c*|v|+1)` ≠ `|v|^(1/3)` for all v. These are different basis functions. **NO MATCH** for 1,2,3,4,5,6,7.

**Candidates with polynomial/rational terms only (v²x, x³, v³, etc.):** These use polynomial basis functions which cannot represent `|v|^(1/3)`. This eliminates most candidates.

Let me specifically check candidates with `|v|` terms or fractional powers:

- **Candidate 3:** Has `v*Abs(v)**c9`. Setting c9 = -2/3 gives `v*|v|^(-2/3)` = `sign(v)*|v|^(1/3)` ≠ `|v|^(1/3)`. **NO MATCH**

- **Candidates with `v*Abs(v)`:** This equals `sign(v)*v² = v*|v|`, not `|v|^(1/3)`. **NO MATCH**

- **Candidates with `v*cos(t+c)`, `x*cos(t+c)`, `exp(t)` terms:** These introduce basis functions not in GT and cannot be zeroed while maintaining the required terms. Most can be zeroed by setting coefficients to 0, but then they lack `x*|v|^(1/3)`.

Going through all candidates systematically: None of them contain the term `x*|v|^(1/3)` or any combination that could produce it. The `log` candidates have `x*log(c|v|+1)` which is fundamentally different. The `|v|^c` candidates have it multiplied by `v` not `x`. Candidate 3 has `v*|v|^c9` which gives `sign(v)*|v|^(c9+1)` - even if we could get `|v|^(1/3)`, it's multiplied by `sign(v)` not `x`.

No candidate can exactly reproduce `x*|v|^(1/3)`.
